  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/929523118" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16077597-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582900704p2/16077597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/929523118?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/12594534-waiting-for-the-barbarians">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=eyes-on-the-prize">eyes-on-the-prize</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer929523118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4760423284712148354">While writing this review the commemoration of the assassination attempt on Hitler is held in Berlin. On this day, 70 years ago, world's history could have taken a turn for the better, but unfortunately the assassination failed. The people involved were executed on that same evening. Needless to say this took place without a charge or trial. But today we remember not only the group of Graf von Stauffenberg, but all resisters and dissidents of the Nazi terror, be they individuals of culture, chur</span>
  <span id="freeText4760423284712148354" style="display:none">While writing this review the commemoration of the assassination attempt on Hitler is held in Berlin. On this day, 70 years ago, world's history could have taken a turn for the better, but unfortunately the assassination failed. The people involved were executed on that same evening. Needless to say this took place without a charge or trial. But today we remember not only the group of Graf von Stauffenberg, but all resisters and dissidents of the Nazi terror, be they individuals of culture, church, labor/middle class, or aristocracy, or groups like the White Rose, the Swing-Kids or the Edelweiss Pirates.<br><br>What has all this have to do with this book? The main character in this story also becomes a resistance fighter. In his capacity as a magistrate of a border-line settlement, he is confronted with the alleged impending attack by the so-called <em>barbarians</em>. When he witnesses how the henchmen of <em>Empire</em>[sic; throughout the book this word is used without article] mistreat their prisoners, a strong resentment grows in him against this rogue regime. After he helped a "barbarian" woman to return to her people, he is declared "enemy of the state" and must learn the hard way what it means to fall from grace.<br><br>This was my second novel by J.M.Coetzee (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6192.Disgrace" title="Disgrace by J.M. Coetzee" rel="nofollow noopener">Disgrace</a> was the first) and again I am impressed by the prose of this author. Despite the scarce descriptions in many parts of this story, he managed to create vivid characters. Their sufferings put tears in my eyes, and their actions let my jugulars swell. Neither the place, nor the time is mentioned and the people remain nameless (with three exceptions). In parts I would compare the book with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984" title="1984 by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">1984</a>, although I liked the ending of Orwell's masterpiece better. Here the ending is one of several possible, but also not the worst. Some allegories remain unclear to me (I'm not well-versed in the Bible), but that does not detract from the overall impression. I would like to ask the author two or three questions, but perhaps they'll answer themselves on my second reading.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>. </span>

<div id="review_1009384096" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1009384096" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1009384096?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099465935" href="/book/show/237040.Waiting_for_the_Barbarians">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=english-literature">english-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=nomadism">nomadism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=south-africa">south-africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1009384096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10378574435715934021">What a powerful piece of prose! The main character is an anti-hero, a magistrate in an outpost of an Empire; he's been there for twenty years and has elapsed into a kind of routine. But then suddenly an army-kolonel turns up who wants to combat the barbarians at the other side of the frontier. The magistrate registers the sudden indications of torture and injustice, does not understand why simple nomads are declared enemies; his fascination focusses especially on a blind and cripple nomad-girl. </span>
  <span id="freeText10378574435715934021" style="display:none">What a powerful piece of prose! The main character is an anti-hero, a magistrate in an outpost of an Empire; he's been there for twenty years and has elapsed into a kind of routine. But then suddenly an army-kolonel turns up who wants to combat the barbarians at the other side of the frontier. The magistrate registers the sudden indications of torture and injustice, does not understand why simple nomads are declared enemies; his fascination focusses especially on a blind and cripple nomad-girl. Of course it doesn't end well, but the end is kept open.<br /><br />In this novel Coetzee immediately dives very deep into the human soul; putting the struggle for humanity and civilisation against the "barbaric" attitude of the military; but it is not a black-and-white-picture (although Coetzee flirts with the theme of the noble savage); even the magistrate knows very well he too personifies some dark aspects of civilization. <br /><br />Looking at the year of publication (1980) there's a temptation to think of this novel as a work of resistance against the South African apartheidsregime; this kind of reading certainly is relevant, but Coetzee has superseded this specific context and brings a broader humanistic story: how thin is the line between civilization and barbarism!<br /><br />Also from a stylistic point of view, this is great literature. The description of the torture and humiliation of the magistrate focusses on the bodily deterioration, and reminds me of Yourcenar's "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/953435.L__uvre_au_noir" title="L'Œuvre au noir by Marguerite Yourcenar" rel="nofollow noopener">L'Œuvre au noir</a>; and then of course the setting (a garnison's town near a desert) inevitably calls into memory Buzzati's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83017.The_Tartar_Steppe" title="The Tartar Steppe by Dino Buzzati" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tartar Steppe</a>. Captivating reading and a ever so relevant warning! (3.5 stars)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10378574435715934021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3417786409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7736681009662574325">A magistrate in a garrison on the frontier of an unnamed empire narrates this shortish novel (about 150p) and Coetzee packs so much in to think about. Isolation and loneliness. The treatment of native peoples, colonialism in general, what is justice. The narrator comes to wonder which side the barbarians are actually on. Skilfully written, the message is powerful.<br/>I decided to read this after watching a trailer for a film based on this book the other day. I’ll be interested to see how well they d</span>
  <span id="freeText7736681009662574325" style="display:none">A magistrate in a garrison on the frontier of an unnamed empire narrates this shortish novel (about 150p) and Coetzee packs so much in to think about. Isolation and loneliness. The treatment of native peoples, colonialism in general, what is justice. The narrator comes to wonder which side the barbarians are actually on. Skilfully written, the message is powerful.<br />I decided to read this after watching a trailer for a film based on this book the other day. I’ll be interested to see how well they do it.<br /></span>

<div id="review_3419809283" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3419809283" />
    <a title="Doug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38333052-doug"><img alt="Doug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1561866244p2/38333052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3419809283?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug" href="/user/show/38333052-doug">Doug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143116929" href="/book/show/7948352-waiting-for-the-barbarians">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3419809283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12713257665853294089">I’d be lying if I told you this was an overly exciting book, but nevertheless Coetzee paints a fascinating portrait of civilization with nothing but a small village for his setting. Here we see civilization in all its ridiculousness, tedium, decadence, and cruelty. Anyone who thinks that wars and acts of conquest are a noble or black and white affair should read this, and I would hope it changes their minds.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/762789929" />
    <a title="Lisajean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22100142-lisajean"><img alt="Lisajean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599969279p2/22100142.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/762789929?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisajean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisajean" href="/user/show/22100142-lisajean">Lisajean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22100142-lisajean?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22100142-lisajean?shelf=south-africa">south-africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22100142-lisajean?shelf=1001-book-list">1001-book-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22100142-lisajean?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer762789929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11202150340478539452">Coetzee is a good writer, but this book was underwhelming. His point about the barbarians was obvious from the very beginning and the narrator was too wishy-washy to make any impression on me. This strikes me as the sort of literature that people like because it is so easy to understand while also being self-consciously literary, whereas I prefer writing that is more complex and less conventional.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1057979781" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1057979781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15736167632758340017">Who is the real barbarian, the colonizer or the less developed colonized? I guess this was the main theme of the book for me. <br/><br/>Waiting for the Barbarian is very powerful book but i enjoyed more Disgrace by the same author.<br/><br/>I observed that there are a few common themes in both books such as:<br/><br/>- the "disgrace of getting old". Both main characters are past their youth and are horrified by the way their body is getting older<br/>- violence and rape<br/>- a father witnesses atrocities being done to their daughter </span>
  <span id="freeText15736167632758340017" style="display:none">Who is the real barbarian, the colonizer or the less developed colonized? I guess this was the main theme of the book for me. <br /><br />Waiting for the Barbarian is very powerful book but i enjoyed more Disgrace by the same author.<br /><br />I observed that there are a few common themes in both books such as:<br /><br />- the "disgrace of getting old". Both main characters are past their youth and are horrified by the way their body is getting older<br />- violence and rape<br />- a father witnesses atrocities being done to their daughter and is unable to intervene and save her<br />- moral questionable relationships that manage to get the main character into trouble and influence his destiny. <br /></span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer588361555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18355590052546307795"><i>Waiting for the Barbarians</i> is brief but it is like a candle lit at both ends - burning bright and chasing the dark away. The whole history of civilization is inside.<br/>“Where civilization entailed the corruption of barbarian virtues and the creation of dependent people, I decided, I was opposed to civilization.”<br/>As a child loses its innocence growing up so civilization deprives human beings of their innate naturalness.</span>

          </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=kind-of-depressing">kind-of-depressing</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40288418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15757481692621284813">And then there're those days when you're like, "Who gives a <i>shit</i> about <i>distance running,</i> and why would I want to watch anyone run a fucking <i>marathon</i>?"<br/><br/>It didn't even occur to me before starting that I wouldn't completely love this book. And I mean yeah, it's really good or whatever, but who cares? It's good, blah blah blah. It's important and well-written, blah blah blah. So what? I'm not feeling it, so none of that helps me. Tonight on the packed train home, I shut this and shoved it into my poc</span>
  <span id="freeText15757481692621284813" style="display:none">And then there're those days when you're like, "Who gives a <i>shit</i> about <i>distance running,</i> and why would I want to watch anyone run a fucking <i>marathon</i>?"<br /><br />It didn't even occur to me before starting that I wouldn't completely love this book. And I mean yeah, it's really good or whatever, but who cares? It's good, blah blah blah. It's important and well-written, blah blah blah. So what? I'm not feeling it, so none of that helps me. Tonight on the packed train home, I shut this and shoved it into my pocket, then stared straight ahead and imagined driving a nail through my skull while a woman next to me screamed about Jesus Christ's Second Coming at the top of her lungs. Anyway, that's when i knew it was time to give up. I just don't feel like reading this book. Judge me, go ahead, I don't care.<br /><br />Full disclosure: I'm in a really crap mood, and I hate every single book I've tried since finishing <i>Beloved</i> a week ago. So I might come back to this thing, but it won't be soon.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2858240004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2858240004" />
    <a title="Michael Perkins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins"><img alt="Michael Perkins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454487234p2/13298726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2858240004?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Perkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Perkins" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins">Michael Perkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/33395775-waiting-for-the-barbarians">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2858240004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16010307619090073852">“I feel old and tired, I want to sleep. I sleep whenever I can nowadays and, when I wake up, wake reluctantly. Sleep is no longer a healing bath, a recuperation of vital forces, but an oblivion, a nightly brush with annihilation.”<br/><br/>===========================<br/><br/>"Waiting for the Barbarians" is a dark tale of xenophobia. At the edges of the author’s imagined Empire live nomads, commonly referred to as barbarians. The center of the action in this story is a remote outpost where an elderly Magistrate, un</span>
  <span id="freeText16010307619090073852" style="display:none">“I feel old and tired, I want to sleep. I sleep whenever I can nowadays and, when I wake up, wake reluctantly. Sleep is no longer a healing bath, a recuperation of vital forces, but an oblivion, a nightly brush with annihilation.”<br /><br />===========================<br /><br />"Waiting for the Barbarians" is a dark tale of xenophobia. At the edges of the author’s imagined Empire live nomads, commonly referred to as barbarians. The center of the action in this story is a remote outpost where an elderly Magistrate, unnamed, was managing to live out his days in relative peace. He is dutiful in carrying out his tasks, but is haunted by bad health….<br /><br />The paranoid complications that the Imperial police brings to the outpost are not welcome by the Magistrate. Armed forces have come to march out among the nomads in the wilderness to allegedly prevent them from attacking the outpost. The barbarians are herders and fishermen, with no such intentions. An old man and a young boy have been “captured” and brought to town and tortured. The old man dies and the boy is seriously wounded. Colonel Joll, a member of the Empire’s secret service, ''the Third Bureau,'' is convinced that, somehow, he’s got the information he needs to proceed. (Iraq, anyone?).<br /><br />The Magistrate wants to be left alone by the authorities to live his quiet life. He rues the fact that he has chosen to live in the middle of the outpost, where more barbarians have been brought and held, instead of his entitled residence out of the main way.<br /><br />“If I lived in the magistrate’s villa on the quietest street in town, holding sittings of the court on Mondays and Thursdays, going hunting every morning, occupying my evenings in the classics, closing my ears to the activities of this upstart policeman, if I resolved to ride out the bad times, keeping my own counsel, I might cease to feel like a man who, in the grip of the undertow, gives up the fight, stops swimming, and turns his face towards the open sea and death.”<br /><br />The aging Magistrate is a kind of symbol of an aging Empire that will inevitably diminish and fall.<br /> <br />The story picks up in Part III in which the Magistrate decides to return a young “barbarian” girl, who had suffered broken feet and legs and became his sex slave, to her people. Accompanied by two soldiers and a guide, the Magistrate and the girl ride off on horseback on a dangerous journey into distant regions, an ordeal of cold, hunger and fear that critic Irving Howe says is “described with such dazzling vividness that it reminds one of the grueling journeys in T.E. Lawrence's ''Seven Pillars of Wisdom.'' <br /><br />This section is the author’s style at its best in this book….<br /><br />“The sun has risen but gives off no warmth. The wind beats at us across the lake bringing tears to our eyes. In single file, four men and a woman, four pack-animals, the horses persistently backing to the wind and having to be sawed around, we wind away from the walled town into the bare fields…. The wind never lets up. It howls at us across the ice, blowing from nowhere to nowhere, veiling the sky in a cloud of red dust. From the dust there is no hiding: it penetrates our clothing, cakes our skin, sifts into the baggage. We eat with coated tongues, spitting often, our teeth grating. Dust rather than air becomes the medium in which we live. We swim through dust like fish through water.”<br /><br />“Rolling down upon us over the snowy plain is a gigantic black wave. It is still miles away but visibly devouring the earth in its approach. Its crest is lost in the murky clouds. “A storm!” I shout. I have never seen anything so frightening….The storm-wall is not black any more but a chaos of whirling sand and snow and dust. Then all at once the wind rises to a scream, my cap is whirled from my head, and the storm hits us. I am knocked flat on my back.”<br /><br />With his mission accomplished, the Magistrate returns home where he is surprised to be charged with treason and imprisoned. Meanwhile, Joll and his men had chased the barbarians beyond the frontiers of the Empire and were led out far into the desert where the nomads vanished. <br /><br />After this exercise in futility, the troops return dispirited. The Magistrate is released from jail. The imperial forces leave. It’s all been for nothing, driven by a paranoid suspicion of “the other.” The story is a reminder of how fear and prejudice are often used to mobilize people to do evil and, in the process, destroy their own souls and sear their consciences. <br /><br />-----------<br /><br />I have now read two of Coetzee's books. I confess that I find the dominant elements of irascibility and selfishness in his main characters----David Lurie in "Disgrace" and the Magistrate in this book----very irritating. Coetzee is famously reclusive and does not give interviews, but I suspect professional reviewers may be correct in seeing much of the author in these characters, including their opinions. <br /><br />"When the main character expresses his own quavering judgments, one suspects that we are really getting the opinions of Coetzee," avers Irving Howe.<br /><br />=====<br /><br />On this note, I found this review of his book "Youth," by the erudite Lisa, of interest....<br /><br />"I absolutely loathed the main character. There is no other way to describe what I felt, page after page, digging deeper into his psyche filled with pretentious nothingness and arrogance. This makes me wonder what the character meant to the author. Does he reflect Coetzee’s own development? If so, there is a huge amount of prejudice and misogyny in his world view, almost painfully evident in every sentence."<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6200.Youth">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16010307619090073852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5p4W2kDs7zdU9x1CvydTZZsWNFBFvv0ktMEuuzG0sPoFouRPd1RKOwQvckL6eEdaBmOgjtemw+UmpE22go+P1Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WEKL8usndc3qjAAHM0JtAB9GC3je4enPLYvVthnqKNm7fnln3J/QwbpUbwd2HXk/gjOfpkz51w6/7ra7qtEX9g==')})
  }

En eftir það sem vinur hennar gerði í partíinu um jólin upplifir hún aðeins einkennilegan doða og tengslaleysi við sjálfa sig og sína nánustu.\n\nSjálfstýring er grípandi samtímasaga sem fjallar á raunsannan hátt um afleiðingar ofbeldis og leiðina til baka út úr myrku herbergi. Sagan sigraði í samkeppni Forlagsins Nýjar raddir 2020.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1043718050389332430\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4128.J_M_Coetzee"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1550676691p3/4128.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4128.J_M_Coetzee">J.M. Coetzee
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,815
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2519600650448118316"><i>John Maxwell Coetzee</i> is an author and academic from South Africa. He became an Australian citizen in 2006 after relocating there in 2002. A novelist and literary critic as well as a translator, Coetzee has won the Booker Prize twice and was awarded the 2003 Nobel Prize in Literature.</span>

</div>
